<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influences</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">
<ul><li>Cultural values of the <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Zhou_dynasty" title="Western Zhou dynasty">Western Zhou dynasties</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFung2008163_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFung2008163-3">[3]</a></sup></li>

</div></div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr class="note"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Virtually all subsequent <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xun_Kuang" title="Xun Kuang">Xun Kuang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Shouren</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neotaoism" title="Neotaoism">Neotaoists</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Han_Yu" title="Han Yu">Han Yu</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoconfucianism" title="Neoconfucianism">Neoconfucians</a>. Also influenced multiple <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western thinkers</a> including <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a Ginsberg">Allen Ginsberg</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a <p><b>Confucius</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">kən-<span style="font-size:90%">FEW</span>-shəs</i></a>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%94" title="wikt:孔">孔</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%AB" title="wikt:夫">夫</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%90" title="wikt:子">子</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Kǒng Fūzǐ</span></i>, "Master Kǒng"; or commonly <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%94" title="wikt:孔">孔</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%90" title="wikt:子">子</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Kǒngzǐ</span></i>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 551</span> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 479 BCE</span>) was a Chinese philosopher and politician of the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> who is traditionally considered the paragon of Chinese sages. Confucius's teachings and philosophy underpin <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian culture and society</a>, remaining influential across <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius considered himself a transmitter for the values of earlier periods which he claimed had been abandoned in his time. His philosophical teachings, called <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, emphasized personal and governmental morality, correctness of social relationships, justice, kindness, and sincerity. His followers competed with many other schools during the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a> era, only to be <a href="/wiki/Suppression_of_monasteries" title="Suppression of monasteries">suppressed</a> in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalists</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>. After the collapse of Qin and the victory of <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chu-Han_Contention" title="Chu-Han Contention">Chu</a>, Confucius's thoughts received official sanction in the new government. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song</a> dynasties, Confucianism developed into a system known in the West as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>, and later as <a href="/wiki/New_Confucianism" title="New Confucianism">New Confucianism</a>. Confucianism was part of the Chinese social fabric and way of life; to Confucians, everyday life was the arena of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius is traditionally credited with having authored or edited many of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">Chinese classic texts</a>, including all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a>, but modern scholars are cautious of attributing specific assertions to Confucius himself. <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">Aphorisms</a> concerning his teachings were compiled in the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i>, but only many years after his death.
</p><p>Confucius's principles have commonality with Chinese tradition and belief. With <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">filial piety</a>, he championed strong family loyalty, <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">ancestor veneration</a>, and respect of elders by their children and of husbands by their wives, recommending family as a basis for ideal government. He espoused the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">Golden Rule</a>, "Do not do unto others what you do not want done to yourself".
</p>

</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>

</ul>
</li>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Etymology"></span><span class="anchor" id="Name"></span><span class="anchor" id="Titles"></span>
The name "Confucius" is a <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinized</a> form of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a> <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Kǒng Fūzǐ</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔夫子</span></span>, "Master Kong"), and was coined in the late 16th century by the early <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit missionaries to China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENivison1999752_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENivison1999752-26">[26]</a></sup> Confucius's  <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname#Xing" title="Chinese surname">clan name</a> was Kong (<span lang="zh">孔</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a>:</small><span class="Unicode" lang="och" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Old Chinese language">*‍</span><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">kʰˤoŋʔ</span>) and his given name was Qiu (<span lang="zh">丘</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a>:</small><span class="Unicode" lang="och" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Old Chinese language">*‍</span><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[k]ʷʰə</span>). His "<a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a>", a capping (<i>guan</i>: <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">冠</span></span>) given at <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">his coming of age ceremony</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunter201750_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunter201750-27">[27]</a></sup> and by which he would have been known to all but his older family members, was Zhongni (<span lang="zh">仲尼</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a>:</small><span class="Unicode" lang="och" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Old Chinese language">*‍</span><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">N-‍truŋ-‍s nr[əj]</span>), the "Zhòng" indicating that he was the second son in his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENivison1999752_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENivison1999752-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2015133_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2015133-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Biography"></span><span class="anchor" id="History"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="568" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg/300px-Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg/450px-Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg/600px-Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_plain_5c._BC-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lu can be seen in China's northeast.</div></div></div>
<p>It is thought that Confucius was born on September 28, 551 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-birth-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194925_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194925-30">[30]</a></sup> in Zou (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%84%92" title="wikt:鄒">鄒</a></span></span>, in modern <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> province).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194925_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194925-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERainey201016_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERainey201016-31">[31]</a></sup> The area was notionally controlled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Zhou_kings" title="List of Zhou kings">kings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a> but effectively independent under the local lords of <a href="/wiki/Lu_(state)" title="Lu (state)">Lu</a>, who ruled from the nearby city of Qufu. His father <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kong_He" title="Kong He">Kong He</a> (or Shuliang He) was an elderly commandant of the local Lu garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELegge1887260_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELegge1887260-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Family_tree_of_Confucius_in_the_main_line_of_descent" title="Family tree of Confucius in the main line of descent">His ancestry</a> traced back through the <a href="/wiki/Song_(state)" title="Song (state)">dukes of Song</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> which had preceded the Zhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELegge1887259_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELegge1887259-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao199729_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao199729-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao200023_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao200023-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERainey201066_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERainey201066-36">[36]</a></sup> Traditional accounts of Confucius's life relate that Kong He's grandfather had migrated the family from Song to Lu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194926_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194926-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kong He died when Confucius was three years old, and Confucius was raised by his mother <a href="/wiki/Yan_Zhengzai" title="Yan Zhengzai">Yan Zhengzai</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A1%8F" title="wikt:顏">顏</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BE%B5" title="wikt:徵">徵</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%A8" title="wikt:在">在</a></span></span>) in poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confu_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-confu-38">[38]</a></sup> His mother later died at less than 40 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confu_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-confu-38">[38]</a></sup> At age 19 he married <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lady_Qiguan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lady Qiguan (page does not exist)">Lady Qiguan</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">亓官氏</span></span>), and a year later the couple had their first child, their son <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kong_Li&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kong Li (page does not exist)">Kong Li</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔鯉</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confu_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-confu-38">[38]</a></sup> Qiguan and Confucius later had two daughters together, one of whom is thought to have died as a child and one was named Kong Jiao (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔姣</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius was educated at schools for commoners, where he studied and learned the <a href="/wiki/Six_Arts" title="Six Arts">Six Arts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius was born into the class of <i><a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">shi</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">士</span></span>), between the aristocracy and the common people. He is said to have worked in various government jobs during his early 20s, and as a bookkeeper and a caretaker of sheep and horses, using the proceeds to give his mother a proper burial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confu_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-confu-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buryl_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-buryl-41">[41]</a></sup> When his mother died, Confucius (aged 23) is said to have <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China#Mourning" title="Ancestor veneration in China">mourned for three years</a>, as was the tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buryl_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-buryl-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career">Political career</span></h3>
<p>In Confucius's time, the state of <a href="/wiki/Lu_(state)" title="Lu (state)">Lu</a> was headed by a ruling ducal house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946274–275_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946274–275-42">[42]</a></sup> Under the duke were <a href="/wiki/Three_Huan" title="Three Huan">three aristocratic families</a>, whose heads bore the title of viscount and held hereditary positions in the Lu bureaucracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275-43">[43]</a></sup> The Ji family held the position "Minister over the Masses", who was also the "Prime Minister"; the Meng family held the position "Minister of Works"; and the Shu family held the position "Minister of War".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275-43">[43]</a></sup> In the winter of 505 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>, Yang Hu—a retainer of the Ji family—rose up in rebellion and seized power from the Ji family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275-43">[43]</a></sup> However, by the summer of 501 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>, the three hereditary families had succeeded in expelling Yang Hu from Lu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275-43">[43]</a></sup> By then, Confucius had built up a considerable reputation through his teachings, while the families came to see the value of proper conduct and righteousness, so they could achieve loyalty to a legitimate government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276-44">[44]</a></sup> Thus, that year (501 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>), Confucius came to be appointed to the minor position of governor of a town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276-44">[44]</a></sup> Eventually, he rose to the position of Minister of Crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946275–276-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius desired to return the authority of the state to the duke by dismantling the fortifications of the city—strongholds belonging to the three families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> This way, he could establish a centralized government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> However, Confucius relied solely on diplomacy as he had no military authority himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> In 500 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>, Hou Fan—the governor of Hou—revolted against his lord of the Shu family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> Although the Meng and Shu families unsuccessfully besieged Hou, a loyalist official rose up with the people of Hou and forced Hou Fan to flee to the <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> The situation may have been in favor for Confucius as this likely made it possible for Confucius and his disciples to convince the aristocratic families to dismantle the fortifications of their cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> Eventually, after a year and a half, Confucius and his disciples succeeded in convincing the Shu family to raze the walls of Hou, the Ji family in razing the walls of Bi, and the Meng family in razing the walls of Cheng.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> First, the Shu family led an army towards their city Hou and tore down its walls in 498 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Confucius_and_Laozi%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg/440px-Confucius_and_Laozi%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> (202 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> – 9 <span title="Common Era">CE</span>) <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> depicting Confucius (and <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>), from a tomb of <a href="/wiki/Dongping_County" title="Dongping County">Dongping County</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shandong_province" title="Shandong province">Shandong province</a>, China</div></div></div></div>
<p>Soon thereafter, Gongshan Furao (also known as Gongshan Buniu), a retainer of the Ji family, revolted and took control of the forces at Bi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277–278_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277–278-47">[47]</a></sup> He immediately launched an attack and entered the capital Lu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277_45-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277-45">[45]</a></sup> Earlier, Gongshan had approached Confucius to join him, which Confucius considered as he wanted the opportunity to put his principles into practice but he gave up on the idea in the end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36-46">[46]</a></sup> Confucius disapproved the use of a violent revolution by principle, even though the Ji family dominated the Lu state by force for generations and had exiled the previous duke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194935–36-46">[46]</a></sup> Creel (1949) states that, unlike the rebel Yang Hu before him, Gongshan may have sought to destroy the three hereditary families and restore the power of the duke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194935_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194935-48">[48]</a></sup> However, Dubs (1946) is of the view that Gongshan was encouraged by Viscount Ji Huan to invade the Lu capital in an attempt to avoid dismantling the Bi fortified walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277–278_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946277–278-47">[47]</a></sup> Whatever the situation may have been, Gongshan was considered an upright man who continued to defend the state of Lu, even after he was forced to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194935_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194935-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the revolt by Gongshan, <a href="/wiki/Zhong_You" title="Zhong You">Zhong You</a> had managed to keep the duke and the three viscounts together at the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278-49">[49]</a></sup> Zhong You was one of the disciples of Confucius and Confucius had arranged for him to be given the position of governor by the Ji family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278–279_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946278–279-50">[50]</a></sup> When Confucius heard of the raid, he requested that Viscount Ji Huan allow the duke and his court to retreat to a stronghold on his palace grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup> Thereafter, the heads of the three families and the duke retreated to the Ji's palace complex and ascended the Wuzi Terrace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chin07-30_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-chin07-30-52">[52]</a></sup> Confucius ordered two officers to lead an assault against the rebels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chin07-30_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-chin07-30-52">[52]</a></sup> At least one of the two officers was a retainer of the Ji family, but they were unable to refuse the orders while in the presence of the duke, viscounts, and court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup> The rebels were pursued and defeated at Gu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chin07-30_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-chin07-30-52">[52]</a></sup> Immediately after the revolt was defeated, the Ji family razed the Bi city walls to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chin07-30_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-chin07-30-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The attackers retreated after realizing that they would have to become rebels against the state and their lord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup> Through Confucius' actions, the Bi officials had inadvertently revolted against their own lord, thus forcing Viscount Ji Huan's hand in having to dismantle the walls of Bi (as it could have harbored such rebels) or confess to instigating the event by going against proper conduct and righteousness as an official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup> Dubs (1949) suggests that the incident brought to light Confucius' foresight, practical political ability, and insight into human character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>When it was time to dismantle the city walls of the Meng family, the governor was reluctant to have his city walls torn down and convinced the head of the Meng family not to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chin07-30_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-chin07-30-52">[52]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuozhuan" title="Zuozhuan">Zuozhuan</a></i> recalls that the governor advised against razing the walls to the ground as he said that it made Cheng vulnerable to the <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi state</a> and cause the destruction of the Meng family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup> Even though Viscount Meng Yi gave his word not to interfere with an attempt, he went back on his earlier promise to dismantle the walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946279-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 498 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>, Duke Ding personally went with an army to lay siege to Cheng in an attempt to raze its walls to the ground, but he did not succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946280_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946280-53">[53]</a></sup> Thus, Confucius could not achieve the idealistic reforms that he wanted including restoration of the legitimate rule of the duke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDubs1946280–281_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDubs1946280–281-54">[54]</a></sup> He had made powerful enemies within the state, especially with Viscount Ji Huan, due to his successes so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubs46-281_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-dubs46-281-55">[55]</a></sup> According to accounts in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuozhuan" title="Zuozhuan">Zuozhuan</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i>, Confucius departed his homeland in 497 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> after his support for the failed attempt of dismantling the fortified city walls of the powerful Ji, Meng, and Shu families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiegel198613_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiegel198613-56">[56]</a></sup> He left the state of Lu without resigning, remaining in self-exile and unable to return as long as Viscount Ji Huan was alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubs46-281_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-dubs46-281-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile">Exile</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Journey_of_Confucius.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Journey_of_Confucius.png/300px-Journey_of_Confucius.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Journey_of_Confucius.png/450px-Journey_of_Confucius.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Journey_of_Confucius.png/600px-Journey_of_Confucius.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Journey_of_Confucius.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the journey of Confucius to various states between 497 BCE and 484 BCE.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i> stated that the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi state</a> was worried that Lu was becoming too powerful while Confucius was involved in the government of the Lu state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENylanWilson201017_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENylanWilson201017-57">[57]</a></sup> According to this account, Qi decided to sabotage Lu's reforms by sending 100 good horses and 80 beautiful dancing girls to the duke of Lu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENylanWilson201017_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENylanWilson201017-57">[57]</a></sup> The duke indulged himself in pleasure and did not attend to official duties for three days. Confucius was disappointed and resolved to leave Lu and seek better opportunities, yet to leave at once would expose the misbehavior of the duke and therefore bring public humiliation to the ruler Confucius was serving. Confucius therefore waited for the duke to make a lesser mistake. Soon after, the duke neglected to send to Confucius a portion of the sacrificial meat that was his due according to custom, and Confucius seized upon this pretext to leave both his post and the Lu state.
</p><p>After Confucius's resignation, he began a long journey or set of journeys around the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_states" title="Ancient Chinese states">principality states</a> of north-east and central China including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wei_(Spring_and_Autumn_Period)" title="Wei (Spring and Autumn Period)">Wey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Song_(state)" title="Song (state)">Song</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zheng_(state)" title="Zheng (state)">Zheng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cao_(state)" title="Cao (state)">Cao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chen_(state)" title="Chen (state)">Chen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cai_(state)" title="Cai (state)">Cai</a> (and a failed attempt to go to <a href="/wiki/Jin_(Chinese_state)" title="Jin (Chinese state)">Jin</a>). At the courts of these states, he expounded his political beliefs but did not see them implemented. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_home">Return home</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confuciustombqufu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Confuciustombqufu.jpg/170px-Confuciustombqufu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Confuciustombqufu.jpg/255px-Confuciustombqufu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Confuciustombqufu.jpg/340px-Confuciustombqufu.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confuciustombqufu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of Confucius in <a href="/wiki/Cemetery_of_Confucius" title="Cemetery of Confucius">Kong Lin cemetery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shandong_Province" title="Shandong Province">Shandong Province</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuozhuan" title="Zuozhuan">Zuozhuan</a></i>, Confucius returned home to his native Lu when he was 68, after he was invited to do so by Ji Kangzi, the chief minister of Lu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-return_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-return-59">[59]</a></sup> The <i>Analects</i> depict him spending his last years teaching 72 or 77 <a href="/wiki/Disciples_of_Confucius" title="Disciples of Confucius">disciples</a> and transmitting the old wisdom via a set of texts called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During his return, Confucius sometimes acted as an advisor to several government officials in Lu, including Ji Kangzi, on matters including governance and crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-return_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-return-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Burdened by the loss of both his son and his favorite disciples, he died at the age of 71 or 72 from natural causes. Confucius was buried in <a href="/wiki/Cemetery_of_Confucius" title="Cemetery of Confucius">Kong Lin cemetery</a> which lies in the historical part of <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a> in the Shandong Province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The original tomb erected there in memory of Confucius on the bank of the <a href="/wiki/Si_River" title="Si River">Sishui River</a> had the shape of an axe. In addition, it has a raised brick platform at the front of the memorial for offerings such as <a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">sandalwood</a> incense and fruit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Xuanyuan_teaching" title="Xuanyuan teaching">Xuanyuanism</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Shengdao" title="Shengdao">Shengdao</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Confucianism" title="Template:Confucianism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Confucianism" title="Template talk:Confucianism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Confucianism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a></div>
<p>Although Confucianism is often followed in a religious manner by the Chinese, many argue that its values are secular and that it is, therefore, less a religion than a secular morality. Proponents argue, however, that despite the secular nature of Confucianism's teachings, it is based on a worldview that is religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Confucianism discusses elements of the <a href="/wiki/Diyu" title="Diyu">afterlife</a> and views concerning <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Heaven</a>, but it is relatively unconcerned with some spiritual matters often considered essential to religious thought, such as the nature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_soul" title="Chinese soul">souls</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i>, Confucius presents himself as a "transmitter who invented nothing". He puts the greatest emphasis on the importance of study, and it is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese character</a> for study (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%B8" title="wikt:學">學</a>) that opens the text. Far from trying to build a systematic or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_formalism" title="Religious formalism">formalist</a> theory, he wanted his disciples to master and internalize older classics, so that their deep thought and thorough study would allow them to relate the moral problems of the present to past political events (as recorded in the <i>Annals</i>) or the past expressions of commoners' feelings and noblemen's reflections (as in the poems of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi_Jing" title="Shi Jing">Book of Odes</a></i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_ethics" title="Confucian ethics">Confucian ethics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rongo_Analects_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/220px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/330px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/440px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rongo_Analects_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>One of the deepest teachings of Confucius may have been the superiority of personal exemplification over explicit rules of behavior. His moral teachings emphasized self-cultivation, emulation of moral exemplars, and the attainment of skilled judgment rather than knowledge of rules. Confucian ethics may, therefore, be considered a type of <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">virtue ethics</a>. His teachings rarely rely on reasoned argument, and ethical ideals and methods are conveyed indirectly, through <a href="/wiki/Allusion" title="Allusion">allusion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Innuendo" title="Innuendo">innuendo</a>, and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tautology_(rhetoric)" title="Tautology (rhetoric)">tautology</a>. His teachings require examination and context to be understood. A good example is found in this famous anecdote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh" style="font-size: 110%;">廄焚。子退朝，曰：傷人乎？不問馬。</span></span>
</p><p>
When the stables were burnt down, on returning from court Confucius said, "Was anyone hurt?" He did not ask about the horses.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>Analects</i> X.11 (tr. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waley" title="Arthur Waley">Waley</a>), 10–13 (tr. <a href="/wiki/James_Legge" title="James Legge">Legge</a>), or X-17 (tr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.C._Lau" title="D.C. Lau">Lau</a>)</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>By not asking about the horses, Confucius demonstrates that the sage values human beings over property (which animals seem to represent in this example); readers are led to reflect on whether their response would follow Confucius's and to pursue self-improvement if it would not have.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>One of his teachings was a variant of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">Golden Rule</a>, sometimes called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_Rule" title="Silver Rule">Silver Rule</a>" owing to its negative form:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh" style="font-size: 110%;">子貢問曰：有一言而可以終身行之者乎？子曰：其恕乎！己所不欲、勿施於人。</span></span>
</p><p>
Zi Gong [a disciple] asked: "Is there any one word that could guide a person throughout life?" The Master replied: "How about 'reciprocity'! Never impose on others what you would not choose for yourself."</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>Analects</i> XV.24, tr. David Hinton</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Often overlooked in Confucian ethics are the virtues to the self: sincerity and the cultivation of knowledge. Virtuous action towards others begins with virtuous and sincere thought, which begins with knowledge. A virtuous disposition without knowledge is susceptible to corruption, and virtuous action without sincerity is not true righteousness. Cultivating knowledge and sincerity is also important for one's own sake; the superior person loves learning for the sake of learning and righteousness for the sake of righteousness.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Confucian theory of ethics as exemplified in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Confucian)" title="Li (Confucian)">lǐ</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">禮</span></span>) is based on three important conceptual aspects of life: (a) ceremonies associated with sacrifice to ancestors and deities of various types, (b) social and political institutions, and (c) the etiquette of daily behavior. Some believed that <i>lǐ</i> originated from the heavens, but Confucius stressed the development of <i>lǐ</i> through the actions of sage leaders in human history. His discussions of <i>lǐ</i> seem to redefine the term to refer to all actions committed by a person to build the ideal society, rather than those simply conforming with canonical standards of ceremony.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the early Confucian tradition, <i>lǐ</i> was doing the proper thing at the proper time; balancing between maintaining existing norms to perpetuate an ethical social fabric, and violating them in order to accomplish ethical good. Training in the <i>lǐ</i> of past sages, cultivates virtues in people that include ethical judgment about when <i>lǐ</i> must be adapted in light of situational contexts.
</p><p>In Confucianism, the concept of <i>li</i> is closely related to <i>yì</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">義</span></span>), which is based upon the idea of reciprocity. <i>Yì</i> can be translated as <a href="/wiki/Righteousness" title="Righteousness">righteousness</a>, though it may simply mean what is ethically best to do in a certain context. The term contrasts with action done out of <a href="/wiki/Selfishness" title="Selfishness">self-interest</a>. While pursuing one's own self-interest is not necessarily bad, one would be a better, more righteous person if one's life was based upon following a path designed to enhance the greater good. Thus an outcome of <i>yì</i> is doing the right thing for the right reason.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Just as action according to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Confucian)" title="Li (Confucian)">lǐ</a></i> should be adapted to conform to the aspiration of adhering to <i>yì</i>, so <i>yì</i> is linked to the core value of <i>rén</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">仁</span></span>).<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ren_(Confucianism)" title="Ren (Confucianism)">Rén</a></i> consists of five basic virtues: seriousness, generosity, sincerity, diligence, and kindness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonevac09-40_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonevac09-40-62">[62]</a></sup> <i>Rén</i> is the virtue of perfectly fulfilling one's responsibilities toward others, most often translated as "benevolence" or "humaneness"; translator <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waley" title="Arthur Waley">Arthur Waley</a> calls it "Goodness" (with a capital <i>G</i>), and other translations that have been put forth include "authoritativeness" and "selflessness". Confucius's moral system was based upon <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">empathy</a> and understanding others, rather than divinely ordained rules. To develop one's spontaneous responses of <i>rén</i> so that these could guide action intuitively was even better than living by the rules of <i>yì</i>. Confucius asserts that virtue is a mean between extremes. For example, the properly generous person gives the right amount – not too much and not too little.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonevac09-40_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bonevac09-40-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<p>Confucius's political thought is based upon his ethical thought. He argued that the best government is one that rules through "rites" (<i>lǐ</i>) and people's <a href="/wiki/Natural_morality" title="Natural morality">natural morality</a>, and <i>not</i> by using bribery and coercion. He explained that this is one of the most important analects: "If the people be led by laws, and uniformity sought to be given them by punishments, they will try to avoid the punishment, but have no sense of shame. If they be led by virtue, and uniformity sought to be given them by the rules of propriety, they will have the sense of the shame, and moreover will become good." (<i>Analects</i> 2.3, tr. Legge). This "sense of shame" is an internalisation of <a href="/wiki/Duty" title="Duty">duty</a>, where the punishment precedes the evil action, instead of following it in the form of laws as in <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Confucius looked nostalgically upon earlier days, and urged the Chinese, particularly those with political power, to model themselves on earlier examples. In times of division, chaos, and endless wars between feudal states, he wanted to restore the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">天命</span></span>) that could unify the "world" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">天下</span></span>, "all under Heaven") and bestow peace and prosperity on the people. Because his vision of personal and social perfections was framed as a revival of the ordered society of earlier times, Confucius is often considered a great proponent of <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a>, but a closer look at what he proposes often shows that he used (and perhaps twisted) past institutions and rites to push a new political agenda of his own: a revival of a unified royal state, whose rulers would succeed to power on the basis of their moral merits instead of lineage. These would be rulers devoted to their people, striving for personal and social <a href="/wiki/Perfection" title="Perfection">perfection</a>, and such a ruler would spread his own virtues to the people instead of imposing proper behavior with laws and rules.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>While Confucius supported the idea of government ruling by a virtuous king, his ideas contained a number of elements to limit the power of rulers. He argued for representing <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> in language, and <a href="/wiki/Honesty" title="Honesty">honesty</a> was of paramount importance. Even in <a href="/wiki/Facial_expression" title="Facial expression">facial expression</a>, truth must always be represented.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Confucius believed that if a ruler is to lead correctly, by action, that orders would be unnecessary in that others will follow the proper actions of their ruler. In discussing the relationship between a king and his subject (or a father and his son), he underlined the need to give due respect to superiors. This demanded that the subordinates must advise their superiors if the superiors are considered to be taking a course of action that is wrong. Confucius believed in ruling by example, if you lead correctly, orders by force or punishment are not necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_poetry">Music and poetry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ritual_Music_System" title="Ritual Music System">Ritual Music System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manuscript_from_Shanghai_Museum_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="378" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Manuscript_from_Shanghai_Museum_1.jpg/220px-Manuscript_from_Shanghai_Museum_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/Manuscript_from_Shanghai_Museum_1.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manuscript_from_Shanghai_Museum_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Shijing</a> or Classic of Poetry</div></div></div>
<p>Confucius heavily promoted the use of music with rituals or the rites order. The scholar <a href="/wiki/Li_Zehou" title="Li Zehou">Li Zehou</a> argued that Confucianism is based on the idea of rites. Rites serve as the starting point for each individual and that these sacred social functions allow each person's human nature to be harmonious with reality. Given this, Confucius believed that "music is the harmonization of heaven and earth; the rites is the order of heaven and earth". Thus the application of music in rites creates the order that makes it possible for society to prosper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Confucian approach to music was heavily inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Shijing</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Music" title="Classic of Music">Classic of Music</a>, which was said to be the sixth Confucian classic until it was lost during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>. The Shijing serves as one of the current Confucian classics and is a book on poetry that contains a diversified variety of poems as well as folk songs. Confucius is traditionally ascribed with compiling these classics within his school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In the Analects, Confucius described the importance of the art in the development of society: <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>The Master said, "My children, why do you not study the Book of Poetry?<br/>
"The Odes serve to stimulate the mind.<br/>
"They may be used for purposes of self-contemplation.<br/>
"They teach the art of sociability.<br/>
"They show how to regulate feelings of resentment.<br/>
"From them you learn the more immediate duty of serving one’s father, and the remoter one of serving one’s prince.<br/>
"From them we become largely acquainted with the names of birds, beasts, and plants."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png"
