<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg/220px-Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg/330px-Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg/440px-Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Conseil_Etat_Confucius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Confucius together with <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> among the greatest legislators of the past, by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Mauzaisse" title="Jean-Baptiste Mauzaisse">Jean-Baptiste Mauzaisse</a> (1827), <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Palace" title="Louvre Palace">Louvre Palace</a></div></div></div>
<p>Confucius's teachings were later turned into an elaborate set of rules and practices by his numerous disciples and followers, who organized his teachings into the Analects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnechtgesShih2010645_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnechtgesShih2010645-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimCsikszentmihalyi201025_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimCsikszentmihalyi201025-68">[68]</a></sup> Confucius's disciples and his only grandson, <a href="/wiki/Zisi" title="Zisi">Zisi</a>, continued his philosophical school after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> These efforts spread Confucian ideals to students who then became officials in many of the royal courts in China, thereby giving Confucianism the first wide-scale test of its <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Two of Confucius's most famous later followers emphasized radically different aspects of his teachings. In the centuries after his death, <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孟子</span></span>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xun_Zi" title="Xun Zi">Xun Zi</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">荀子</span></span>) both composed important teachings elaborating in different ways on the fundamental ideas associated with Confucius. <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> (4th century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>) articulated the innate goodness in human beings as a source of the ethical intuitions that guide people towards <i>rén</i>, <i>yì</i>, and <i>lǐ</i>, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xun_Zi" title="Xun Zi">Xun Zi</a> (3rd century <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>) underscored the realistic and materialistic aspects of Confucian thought, stressing that morality was inculcated in society through tradition and in individuals through training. In time, their writings, together with the <i>Analects</i> and other core texts came to constitute the philosophical corpus of Confucianism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>This realignment in Confucian thought was parallel to the development of <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>, which saw filial piety as self-interest and not a useful tool for a ruler to create an effective state.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A disagreement between these two political philosophies came to a head in 223 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> when the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin state</a> conquered all of China. <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a>, Prime Minister of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, convinced <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> to abandon the Confucians' recommendation of awarding fiefs akin to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou Dynasty</a> before them which he saw as being against to the Legalist idea of centralizing the state around the ruler. When the Confucian advisers pressed their point, Li Si had many Confucian scholars killed and their books burned—considered a huge blow to the philosophy and Chinese scholarship.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Under the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> dynasties, Confucian ideas gained even more widespread prominence. Under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Wudi</a>, the works of Confucius were made the official imperial philosophy and required reading for civil service examinations in 140 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span> which was continued nearly unbroken until the end of the 19th century. As <a href="/wiki/Mohism" title="Mohism">Mohism</a> lost support by the time of the Han, the main philosophical contenders were Legalism, which Confucian thought somewhat absorbed, the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>, whose focus on more spiritual ideas kept it from direct conflict with Confucianism, and the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> religion, which gained acceptance during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_and_Northern_Dynasties" title="Southern and Northern Dynasties">Southern and Northern Dynasties</a> era. Both Confucian ideas and Confucian-trained officials were relied upon in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> and even the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> distrusted handing over provincial control to them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>, the scholar <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200 <span title="Common Era">CE</span>) added ideas from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> into Confucianism. In his life, Zhu Xi was largely ignored, but not long after his death, his ideas became the new orthodox view of what Confucian texts actually meant. Modern historians view Zhu Xi as having created something rather different and call his way of thinking <i><a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a></i>. Neo-Confucianism held sway in China, Japan, Korea, and Vietnam until the 19th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/220px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/330px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/440px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Confucius, Philosopher of the Chinese</i>, published by <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit missionaries</a> at Paris in 1687.</div></div></div>
<p>The works of Confucius were first translated into European languages by <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit missionaries</a> in the 16th century during the late <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>. The first known effort was by <a href="/wiki/Michele_Ruggieri" title="Michele Ruggieri">Michele Ruggieri</a>, who returned to Italy in 1588 and carried on his translations while residing in <a href="/wiki/Salerno" title="Salerno">Salerno</a>. <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> started to report on the thoughts of Confucius, and a team of Jesuits—<a href="/wiki/Prospero_Intorcetta" title="Prospero Intorcetta">Prospero Intorcetta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Couplet" title="Philippe Couplet">Philippe Couplet</a>, and two others—published a translation of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_canon" title="Confucian canon">Confucian works</a> and an overview of <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">Chinese history</a> in Paris in 1687.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker77-25_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-parker77-25-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_No%C3%ABl_(missionary)" title="François Noël (missionary)">François Noël</a>, after failing to persuade <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_XI" title="Pope Clement XI">Clement<span class="nowrap"> </span>XI</a> that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestral_veneration" title="Chinese ancestral veneration">Chinese veneration of ancestors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucius</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Rites_controversy" title="Chinese Rites controversy">did not constitute idolatry</a>, completed the Confucian canon at <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> in 1711, with more scholarly treatments of the other works and the first translation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mengzi_(book)" title="Mengzi (book)">collected works</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> It is thought that such works had considerable importance on European thinkers of the period, particularly among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deists" title="Deists">Deists</a> and other philosophical groups of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> who were interested by the integration of the system of morality of Confucius into <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker77-25_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-parker77-25-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobson2004194–195_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobson2004194–195-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern era Confucian movements, such as <a href="/wiki/New_Confucianism" title="New Confucianism">New Confucianism</a>, still exist, but during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>, Confucianism was frequently attacked by leading figures in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a>. This was partially a continuation of the condemnations of Confucianism by intellectuals and activists in the early 20th century as a cause of the ethnocentric close-mindedness and refusal of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> to modernize that led to the tragedies that befell China in the 19th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Confucius's works are studied by scholars in many other Asian countries, particularly those in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_cultural_sphere" title="Chinese cultural sphere">Chinese cultural sphere</a>, such as Korea, Japan, and Vietnam. Many of those countries still hold the traditional memorial ceremony every year.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetans" title="Tibetans">Tibetans</a>, Confucius is often worshipped as a holy king and master of magic, divination and astrology. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhists" title="Tibetan Buddhists">Tibetan Buddhists</a> see him as learning divination from the Buddha <a href="/wiki/Manjushri" title="Manjushri">Manjushri</a> (and that knowledge subsequently reaching Tibet through <a href="/wiki/Princess_Wencheng" title="Princess Wencheng">Princess Wencheng</a>), while <a href="/wiki/Bon" title="Bon">Bon</a> practitioners see him as being a reincarnation of <a href="/wiki/Tonpa_Shenrab_Miwoche" title="Tonpa Shenrab Miwoche">Tonpa Shenrab Miwoche</a>, the legendary founder of Bon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Muslim_Community" title="Ahmadiyya Muslim Community">Ahmadiyya Muslim Community</a> believes Confucius was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">Divine Prophet</a> of God, as were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lao-Tzu" title="Lao-Tzu">Lao-Tzu</a> and other eminent Chinese personages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7853_Confucius" title="7853 Confucius">Asteroid 7853</a>, "Confucius", was named after the Chinese thinker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disciples">Disciples</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Disciples_of_Confucius" title="Disciples of Confucius">Disciples of Confucius</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_(%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1916" data-file-width="2162" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg/220px-The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg/330px-The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg/440px-The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_%28%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Classic_of_Filial_Piety_(%E9%96%8B%E5%AE%97%E6%98%8E%E7%BE%A9%E7%AB%A0_%E7%95%AB).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zengzi" title="Zengzi">Zengzi</a> (right) kneeling before Confucius (center), as depicted in a painting from the <i>Illustrations of the <a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Filial_Piety" title="Classic of Filial Piety">Classic of Filial Piety</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>Confucius began teaching after he turned 30, and taught more than 3,000 students in his life, about 70 of whom were considered outstanding. His disciples and the early Confucian community they formed became the most influential intellectual force in the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201386_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201386-77">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> dedicated a chapter in his <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> to the biographies of Confucius's disciples, accounting for the influence they exerted in their time and afterward. Sima Qian recorded the names of 77 disciples in his collective biography, while <i><a href="/wiki/Kongzi_Jiayu" title="Kongzi Jiayu">Kongzi Jiayu</a></i>, another early source, records 76, not completely overlapping. The two sources together yield the names of 96 disciples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201387_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201387-78">[78]</a></sup> Twenty-two of them are mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i>, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Mencius_(book)" title="Mencius (book)">Mencius</a></i> records 24.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194930_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194930-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius did not charge any tuition, and only requested a symbolic gift of a bundle of dried meat from any prospective student. According to his disciple <a href="/wiki/Duanmu_Ci" title="Duanmu Ci">Zigong</a>, his master treated students like doctors treated patients and did not turn anybody away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201387_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201387-78">[78]</a></sup> Most of them came from <a href="/wiki/Lu_(state)" title="Lu (state)">Lu</a>, Confucius's home state, with 43 recorded, but he accepted students from all over China, with six from the state of <a href="/wiki/Wey_(state)" title="Wey (state)">Wey</a> (such as Zigong), three from <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a>, two each from <a href="/wiki/Chen_(state)" title="Chen (state)">Chen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi</a>, and one each from <a href="/wiki/Cai_(state)" title="Cai (state)">Cai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Song_(state)" title="Song (state)">Song</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201387_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201387-78">[78]</a></sup> Confucius considered his students' personal background irrelevant, and accepted noblemen, commoners, and even former criminals such as Yan Zhuoju and <a href="/wiki/Gongye_Chang" title="Gongye Chang">Gongye Chang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201388_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201388-80">[80]</a></sup> His disciples from richer families would pay a sum commensurate with their wealth which was considered a ritual donation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen201387_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen201387-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius's favorite disciple was <a href="/wiki/Yan_Hui" title="Yan Hui">Yan Hui</a>, most probably one of the most impoverished of them all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194930_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194930-79">[79]</a></sup> Sima Niu, in contrast to Yan Hui, was from a hereditary noble family hailing from the Song state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194930_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194930-79">[79]</a></sup> Under Confucius's teachings, the disciples became well learned in the principles and methods of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> He often engaged in discussion and debate with his students and gave high importance to their studies in history, poetry, and ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> Confucius advocated loyalty to principle rather than to individual acumen, in which reform was to be achieved by persuasion rather than violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> Even though Confucius denounced them for their practices, the aristocracy was likely attracted to the idea of having trustworthy officials who were studied in morals as the circumstances of the time made it desirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> In fact, the disciple <a href="/wiki/Zhong_You" title="Zhong You">Zilu</a> even died defending his ruler in <a href="/wiki/Wey_(state)" title="Wey (state)">Wey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yang Hu, who was a subordinate of the Ji family, had dominated the Lu government from 505 to 502 and even attempted a coup, which narrowly failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> As a likely consequence, it was after this that the first disciples of Confucius were appointed to government positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932_81-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932-81">[81]</a></sup> A few of Confucius's disciples went on to attain official positions of some importance, some of which were arranged by Confucius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194931_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194931-82">[82]</a></sup> By the time Confucius was 50 years old, the Ji family had consolidated their power in the Lu state over the ruling ducal house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194933_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194933-83">[83]</a></sup> Even though the Ji family had practices with which Confucius disagreed and disapproved, they nonetheless gave Confucius's disciples many opportunities for employment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194933_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194933-83">[83]</a></sup> Confucius continued to remind his disciples to stay true to their principles and renounced those who did not, all the while being openly critical of the Ji family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECreel194932–33_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECreel194932–33-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_portraits">Visual portraits</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1141" data-file-width="644" decoding="async" height="390" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png/220px-%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png/330px-%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png/440px-%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%AD%94%E5%AD%90%E8%81%96%E8%B9%9F%E5%9C%96.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a> (1494–1552), <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>No contemporary painting or sculpture of Confucius survives, and it was only during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> that he was portrayed visually. Carvings often depict his legendary meeting with <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>. Since that time there have been many portraits of Confucius as the ideal philosopher. The oldest known portrait of Confucius has been unearthed in the tomb of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Marquis_of_Haihun" title="Marquis of Haihun">Marquis of Haihun</a> (died 59 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>). The picture was painted on the wooden frame to a polished bronze mirror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In former times, it was customary to have a portrait in <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">Confucius Temples</a>; however, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> (Taizu) of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>, it was decided that the only proper portrait of Confucius should be in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius,_Qufu" title="Temple of Confucius, Qufu">temple</a> in his home town, <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a> in Shandong. In other temples, Confucius is represented by a memorial tablet. In 2006, the China Confucius Foundation commissioned a standard portrait of Confucius based on the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> portrait by <a href="/wiki/Wu_Daozi" title="Wu Daozi">Wu Daozi</a>.
</p><p>The South Wall <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">Frieze</a> in the courtroom of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> depicts Confucius as a teacher of harmony, learning, and virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_portrayals">Fictional portrayals</span></h3>
<p>There have been two film adaptations of Confucius' life: the 1940 film <a href="/wiki/Confucius_(1940_film)" title="Confucius (1940 film)"><i>Confucius</i></a> starring Tang Huaiqiu, and the 2010 film <a href="/wiki/Confucius_(2010_film)" title="Confucius (2010 film)"><i>Confucius</i></a> starring <a href="/wiki/Chow_Yun-fat" title="Chow Yun-fat">Chow Yun-fat</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius,_Ching-hai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg/220px-First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg/330px-First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg/440px-First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius%2C_Ching-hai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_Entrance_Gate_of_the_Temple_of_Confucius,_Ching-hai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First entrance gate of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">Temple of Confucius</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhenhai" title="Zhenhai">Zhenhai</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1963" data-file-width="2951" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg/220px-20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg/330px-20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg/440px-20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20130918-%E5%98%89%E5%AE%9A%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">Temple of Confucius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jiading_District" title="Jiading District">Jiading</a>, now a suburb of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>. The Jiading Temple of Confucius now operates a museum devoted to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_exam" title="Imperial exam">imperial exam</a> formerly administered at the temples.</div></div></div>
<p>Soon after Confucius's death, <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a>, his home town, became a place of devotion and remembrance. The <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> records that it had already become a place of pilgrimage for ministers. It is still a major destination for cultural tourism, and many people visit his grave and the surrounding temples. In Sinic cultures, there are many temples where representations of the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>, and Confucius are found together. There are also many <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">temples</a> dedicated to him, which have been used for Confucian ceremonies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Followers of Confucianism have a tradition of holding spectacular memorial ceremonies of Confucius (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">祭孔</span></span>) every year, using ceremonies that supposedly derived from Zhou Li (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">周禮</span></span>) as recorded by Confucius, on the date of Confucius's birth. In the 20th century, this tradition was interrupted for several decades in mainland China, where the official stance of the Communist Party and the State was that Confucius and Confucianism represented reactionary <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalist</a> beliefs which held that the subservience of the people to the <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocracy</a> is a part of the natural order. All such ceremonies and rites were therefore banned. Only after the 1990s did the ceremony resume. As it is now considered a veneration of Chinese history and tradition, even Communist Party members may be found in attendance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Taiwan, where the Nationalist Party (<a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a>) strongly promoted Confucian beliefs in ethics and behavior, the tradition of the memorial ceremony of Confucius (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">祭孔</span></span>) is supported by the government and has continued without interruption. While not a national holiday, it does appear on all printed calendars, much as <a href="/wiki/Father%27s_Day" title="Father's Day">Father's Day</a> or <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas Day</a> do in the Western world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, a grand-scale memorial ceremony called <a href="/wiki/Seokjeon_Daeje" title="Seokjeon Daeje">Seokjeon Daeje</a> is held twice a year on <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Confucius%27s_birthday&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Confucius's birthday (page does not exist)">Confucius's birthday</a> and the anniversary of his death, at <a href="/wiki/Hyanggyo" title="Hyanggyo">Confucian academies</a> across the country and <a href="/wiki/Sungkyunkwan" title="Sungkyunkwan">Sungkyunkwan</a> in Seoul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants">Descendants</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Family_tree_of_Confucius_in_the_main_line_of_descent" title="Family tree of Confucius in the main line of descent">Family tree of Confucius in the main line of descent</a></div>
<p>Confucius's descendants were repeatedly identified and honored by successive imperial governments with titles of nobility and official posts. They were honored with the rank of a <a href="/wiki/Marquess" title="Marquess">marquis</a> 35 times since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaozu_of_Han" title="Gaozu of Han">Gaozu</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, and they were promoted to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">duke</a> 42 times from the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a> first bestowed the title of "Duke Wenxuan" on Kong Suizhi of the 35th generation. In 1055, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Renzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Renzong of Song">Emperor Renzong of Song</a> first bestowed the title of "<a href="/wiki/Duke_Yansheng" title="Duke Yansheng">Duke Yansheng</a>" on Kong Zongyuan of the 46th generation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song_dynasty" title="Southern Song dynasty">Southern Song dynasty</a>, the Duke Yansheng Kong Duanyou fled south with the Song Emperor to <a href="/wiki/Quzhou" title="Quzhou">Quzhou</a> in Zhejiang, while the newly established <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty (1115–1234)</a> in the north appointed Kong Duanyou's brother Kong Duancao who remained in Qufu as Duke Yansheng.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> From that time up until the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, there were two Duke Yanshengs, one in the north in Qufu and the other in the south at Quzhou. An invitation to come back to Qufu was extended to the southern Duke Yansheng Kong Zhu by the Yuan-dynasty Emperor Kublai Khan. The title was taken away from the southern branch after Kong Zhu rejected the invitation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJpgPA187_187–188]_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJpgPA187_187–188]-95">[95]</a></sup> so the northern branch of the family kept the title of Duke Yansheng. The southern branch remained in Quzhou where they live to this day. Confucius's descendants in Quzhou alone number 30,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (November 2021)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hanlin_Academy" title="Hanlin Academy">Hanlin Academy</a> rank of Wujing boshi <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%BA%94%E7%B6%93%E5%8D%9A%E5%A3%AB" title="zh:五經博士">五經博士</a> was awarded to the southern branch at Quzhou by a Ming Emperor while the northern branch at Qufu held the title Duke Yansheng.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2002_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2002-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJqwujingboshidescendantpgPA188_188]_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJqwujingboshidescendantpgPA188_188]-98">[98]</a></sup> The leader of the southern branch is 孔祥楷 Kong Xiangkai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJpgPA189_189]_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansenKleinMeyer2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidGNlXAwAAQBAJpgPA189_189]-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1351, during the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Toghon_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Toghon Temür">Toghon Temür</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, 53rd-generation descendant Kong Huan (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔浣</span></span>)'s second son Kong Shao (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔昭</span></span>) moved from China to Korea during the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo Dynasty</a>, and was received courteously by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Noguk" title="Queen Noguk">Princess Noguk</a> (the Mongolian-born wife of the future king <a href="/wiki/Gongmin_of_Goryeo" title="Gongmin of Goryeo">Gongmin</a>). After being naturalized as a Korean citizen, he changed the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> of his name from "昭" to "紹" (both pronounced <i>so</i> in Korean),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> married a Korean woman and bore a son (Gong Yeo (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">공여</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hani">孔帤</span></span>), 1329–1397), therefore establishing the Changwon Gong clan (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">창원 공씨</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hani">昌原 孔氏</span></span>), whose ancestral seat was located in <a href="/wiki/Changwon" title="Changwon">Changwon</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Gyeongsang_Province" title="South Gyeongsang Province">South Gyeongsang Province</a>. The clan then received an <a href="/wiki/Korean_nobility" title="Korean nobility">aristocratic rank</a> during the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In 1794, during the reign of King <a href="/wiki/Jeongjo_of_Joseon" title="Jeongjo of Joseon">Jeongjo</a>, the clan then changed its name to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qufu_Kong_clan" title="Qufu Kong clan">Gokbu Gong clan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">곡부 공씨</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hani">曲阜 孔氏</span></span>) in honor of Confucius's birthplace <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">곡부</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a>: <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hani">曲阜</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean" title="Revised Romanization of Korean">RR</a>: <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn">Gokbu</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Famous descendants include actors such as <a href="/wiki/Gong_Yoo" title="Gong Yoo">Gong Yoo</a> (real name Gong Ji-cheol (공지철)) and <a href="/wiki/Gong_Hyo-jin" title="Gong Hyo-jin">Gong Hyo-jin</a> (공효진); and artists such as male idol group <a href="/wiki/B1A4" title="B1A4">B1A4</a> member <a href="/wiki/Gongchan" title="Gongchan">Gongchan</a> (real name Gong Chan-sik (공찬식)), singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Minzy" title="Minzy">Minzy</a> (real name Gong Min-ji (공민지)), as well as her great aunt, traditional folk dancer <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gong_Ok-jin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gong Ok-jin (page does not exist)">Gong Ok-jin</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ko.wikipedia.org/wiki/%EA%B3%B5%EC%98%A5%EC%A7%84" title="ko:공옥진">ko</a>]</span> (공옥진).
</p><p>Despite repeated dynastic change in China, the title of Duke Yansheng was bestowed upon successive generations of descendants until it was abolished by the <a href="/wiki/Nationalist_government" title="Nationalist government">Nationalist government</a> in 1935. The last holder of the title, <a href="/wiki/Kung_Te-cheng" title="Kung Te-cheng">Kung Te-cheng</a> of the 77th generation, was appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrificial_Official_to_Confucius" title="Sacrificial Official to Confucius">Sacrificial Official to Confucius</a>. Kung Te-cheng died in October 2008, and his son, Kung Wei-yi, the 78th lineal descendant, died in 1989. Kung Te-cheng's grandson, <a href="/wiki/Kung_Tsui-chang" title="Kung Tsui-chang">Kung Tsui-chang</a>, the 79th lineal descendant, was born in 1975; his great-grandson, Kung Yu-jen, the 80th lineal descendant, was born in Taipei on January 1, 2006. Te-cheng's sister, Kong Demao, lives in mainland China and has written a book about her experiences growing up at the family estate in Qufu. Another sister, Kong Deqi, died as a young woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demao_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demao-107">[107]</a></sup> Many descendants of Confucius still live in Qufu today.
</p><p>A descendant of Confucius, <a href="/wiki/H._H._Kung" title="H. H. Kung">H. H. Kung</a>, was the <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Premier of the Republic of China">Premier of the Republic of China</a>. One of his sons, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kong_Lingjie&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kong Lingjie (page does not exist)">Kong Lingjie</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%AD%94%E4%BB%A4%E5%82%91" title="zh:孔令傑">zh</a>]</span> (孔令傑), married <a href="/wiki/Debra_Paget" title="Debra Paget">Debra Paget</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-i_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-i-108">[108]</a></sup> who gave birth to Gregory Kung (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">孔德基</span></span>).
</p><p>Confucius's family, the Kongs, have the longest recorded extant pedigree in the world today. The father-to-son family tree, now in its 83rd generation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> has been recorded since the death of Confucius. According to the <a href="/wiki/Confucius_Genealogy_Compilation_Committee" title="Confucius Genealogy Compilation Committee">Confucius Genealogy Compilation Committee</a> (CGCC), he has two million known and registered descendants, and there are an estimated three million in all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yanl_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-yanl-110">[110]</a></sup> Of these, several tens of thousands live outside of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yanl_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-yanl-110">[110]</a></sup> In the 14th century, a Kong descendant went to Korea, where an estimated 34,000 descendants of Confucius live today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yanl_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-yanl-110">[110]</a></sup> One of the main lineages fled from the Kong ancestral home in <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> in the 1940s and eventually settled in Taiwan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demao_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Demao-107">[107]</a></sup> There are also branches of the Kong family who have converted to Islam after marrying Muslim women, in Dachuan in Gansu province in the 1800s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and in 1715 in <a href="/wiki/Xuanwei" title="Xuanwei">Xuanwei</a> in Yunnan province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou-112">[112]</a></sup> Many of the Muslim Confucius descendants are descended from the marriage of Ma Jiaga (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">馬甲尕</span></span>), a Muslim woman, and Kong Yanrong (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">孔彥嶸</span></span>), 59th generation descendant of Confucius in the year 1480, and are found among the <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dongxiang_people" title="Dongxiang people">Dongxiang</a> peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The new genealogy includes the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Kong Dejun (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">孔德軍</span></span>) is a prominent Islamic scholar and Arabist from Qinghai province and a 77th generation descendant of Confucius.
</p><p>Because of the huge interest in the Confucius family tree, there was a project in China to test the DNA of known family members of the collateral branches in mainland China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Among other things, this would allow scientists to identify a common Y chromosome in male descendants of Confucius. If the descent were truly unbroken, father-to-son, since Confucius's lifetime, the males in the family would all have the same Y chromosome as their direct male ancestor, with slight mutations due to the passage of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The aim of the genetic test was to help members of collateral branches in China who lost their genealogical records to prove their descent. However, in 2009, many of the collateral branches decided not to agree to DNA testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seedmagazine_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-seedmagazine-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Sykes" title="Bryan Sykes">Bryan Sykes</a>, professor of genetics at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>, understands this decision: "The Confucius family tree has an enormous cultural significance ... It's not just a scientific question."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seedmagazine_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-seedmagazine-120">[120]</a></sup> The DNA testing was originally proposed to add new members, many of whose family record books were lost during 20th century upheavals, to the Confucian family tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The main branch of the family which fled to Taiwan was never involved in the proposed DNA test at all.
</p><p>In 2013 a DNA test performed on multiple different families who claimed descent from Confucius found that they shared the same Y chromosome as reported by <a href="/wiki/Fudan_University" title="Fudan University">Fudan University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fifth and most recent edition of the Confucius genealogy was printed by the CGCC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhouccgc09_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhouccgc09-123">[123]</a></sup> It was unveiled in a ceremony at Qufu on September 24, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhouccgc09_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhouccgc09-123">[123]</a></sup> Women are now included for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
